=500  \\$aFrancesco Geminiani (1687-1762) was an Italian composer, violinist, teacher, writer on musical performance, and a leading figure in early 18th-century music.
=500  \\$aGeminiani studied under Corelli. He established his reputation as a brilliant performer in England, publishing (1716) his Opus 1 sonatas for violin and continuo (see 07D13), which were famous for their technical difficulty and were widely acclaimed as equal to Corelli’s. His other popular works include concerti grossi, of which his Opus 2 (see 07D14) and Opus 3 (see 07D16) sets became extremely popular in England, holding a place in the repertory well into the next century. His theoretical writings, particularly The Art of Playing on the Violin (1751), had considerable influence, and the latter work remains an important reference on the performance of late Baroque music.

=245  \\$aQuatuors du ballet intitulé Die Geschöpfe des Prometheus$bcomposé & arrangé pour 2 violons, viola & basse. Oeuvre IX
=260  \\$aBerlin$bHummel$c1808
=300  \\$a4 parts (14 +13 +13 + 11 pages)
=500  \\$aThe Creatures of Prometheus op. 43 is a ballet by Ludwig van Beethoven and Salvatore Viganò (libretto and choreography). The first performance took place on 28 March 1801 at the Vienna Hofburg Theatre as a benefit performance for prima ballerina Maria Cassentini, who also danced the leading female role. 
=500  \\$aArrangement for string quartet probably by Franz Alexander Pössinger (1766–1827), an Austrian violinist, composer and arranger, and a contemporary and friend of Beethoven. Pössinger's works for string quartet illustrate in a fascinating way the role of chamber music in canon formation, music publishing and listening culture in early 19th century Europe. The collection of the Beethoven Haus Bonn includes a letter from Beethoven's younger brother Kaspar Karl, who gives Pössinger permission to arrange Beethoven's op. 43 ("The Creatures of Prometheus") for string quartet.

=500  \\$aThis edition, edited by J.J. Hummel, Berlin, has Plate number 1307
Artaria et Comp. Wien, also published in 1803 a version for string quartet "Quatuors pour deux Violons, Viole et Basse tirés du Ballet intitulé Die Geschöphe [sic!] des Prometeus (PlattenNr. 1620), likewise without mentioning the arranger's name.
=500  \\$acontains some pensil remarks

=100  \\$aBerckenhoff, Theodorus Albertus$d1751-1814$4cmp$96119
=245  \\$aJo vivat avec 12 variations pour le violon
=260  \\$aLeiden$bs.n.$c1813
=300  \\$a7 pages
=500  \\$aTheodorus Berckenhoff was an violonist and a freemason who made a name with the arrangement of this students' song
=500  \\$aThe Io vivat is a well-known student song in the Netherlands and Belgium. The Io vivat originated in Leiden during the Batavian Republic. The song is included in many student almanacs and songbooks, especially in the 19th century. Numerous Universities sing the song at the opening of the academic year. Many other song texts have been written to the melody.

=500  \\$aVery little is known about Vincenzo Panerai (1750-1790), other than that he studied composition alongside Luigi Cherubini in Florence. Cherubini is known to have praised the performance of Panerai's music in the latter city in the 1770s. A teacher and author of several didactic works (see 03I10), Panerai published one of the few fingering tables for flute surviving from Italy during the second of the eighteenth century.

=500  \\$aThe Florentine composer Giovanni Marco Rutini (1723-1797) trained at the Conservatorio della Pietà dei Turchini in Naples. He moved to Prague where he joined the group around Locatelli and composed sonatas for harpsichord (including this one?). He then went to Saint Petersburg, where he composed opere buffe on libretti by Goldoni and taught pianoforte to Catherine II. He returned to Florence in the early 1760s.

=041  \\$afre$hita
=100  \\$aGluck, Christoph Willibald$d1714-1787$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q130759$96111
=245  \\$aAlceste.$bTragédie-opéra en trois actes
=260  \\$aParis$aLyon$bBureau d'Abonnement Musical$bCastaud$c[1777]
=300  \\$a[i]-293 pages
=500  \\$aAlceste, Wq. 37 (the later French version is Wq. 44), is an opera by Christoph Willibald Gluck from 1767. The libretto (in Italian) was written by Ranieri de' Calzabigi and based on the play Alcestis by Euripides. The premiere of the Italian version took place on 26 December 1767 at the Burgtheater in Vienna.
=500  \\$aGluck recomposed and lengthened Alceste to a French libretto by François-Louis Gand Le Bland Du Roullet for performances at the Paris Opera, retaining the three-act structure. Hercules was added as a pivotal character in Act III, as was a scene at the Gates of Hell. With the presentations in Paris, Alceste became an essentially new work, the translation from Italian to French necessitating several changes in the musical declamation of text, and certain scenes significantly reorganized to new or altered music.
=534  \\$aFrench version premiered at the Académie royale de musique, Paris, 1776
=610  \0$aOpéra-Comique (Paris, France)$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q872222$920953

=100  \\$aSpohr, Louis$d1784-1859$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q57226$96129

=245  \\$aHistorische Symphonie:$bIm Styl und gesmack vier verschiedener Zeitabschnitte
=250  \\$a1st ed.
=260  \\$aVienna$bMechetti$c[1842]
=300  \\$a[113] pages
=500  \\$aLouis Spohr (1784-1859) was a German composer, violinist and conductor. Highly regarded during his lifetime, Spohr composed ten symphonies, ten operas, eighteen violin concerti, four clarinet concerti, four oratorios, and various works for small ensemble, chamber music, and art songs. Spohr invented the violin chinrest and the orchestral rehearsal mark. His output spans the transition between Classical and Romantic music, but fell into obscurity following his death, when his music was rarely heard. The late 20th century saw a revival of interest in his oeuvre, especially in Europe.
=500  \\$aSpohr's sixth, historicist symphony,was composed in 1839

=500  \\$aJean Balthasar Tricklir (1750-1813) was a French cellist and composer of German descent.
=500  \\$aTricklir wrote a number of cello concertos and sonatas, as well as solo and duet works for cello, however, his works are little known today. He authored two theoretical treatises: 'Le Microcosme Musical' and 'Discours Analytique'. His theoretical studies included analysis of the role of temperature in affecting the sound of musical instruments.

=500  \\$aoriginally published in Paris, 1778
=500  \\$aGerman pianist & composer J.S. Schröter came to England in 1771. Initially organist at the Royal German Chapel, he became a protege of Johann Christian Bach. With connections to court, he became a celebrated pianist. In 1782, after Bach's death, he became music-master to the Queen. A few years after Schröter's death, the married Joseph Haydn met his young widow Rebecca Schroeter, fell in love with her and dedicated his Trios op. 73 to her.

=100  \\$aHandel, George Frideric$d1685-1759$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q7302$94765
=240  \\$aop. 6, HWV 319-330
=245  \\$aTwelve Grand Concertos in Score:$bComposed in the Year 1737
=260  \\$aLondon$bArnold$c1789
=300  \\$a220 pages
=490  \\$aThe Works of G. F. Händel, Ed. in Score$v60-64
=520  \\$aThe Concerti Grossi, Op. 6, or Twelve Grand Concertos, HWV 319–330, are 12 concerti grossi by George Frideric Handel for a concertino trio of two violins and violoncello and a ripieno four-part string orchestra with harpsichord continuo. First published by subscription in London by John Walsh in 1739, in the second edition of 1741 they became Handel's Opus 6. Taking the older concerto da chiesa and concerto da camera of Arcangelo Corelli as models, rather than the later three-movement Venetian concerto of Antonio Vivaldi favoured by Johann Sebastian Bach, they were written to be played during performances of Handel's oratorios and odes. Despite the conventional model, Handel incorporated in the movements the full range of his compositional styles, including trio sonatas, operatic arias, French overtures, Italian sinfonias, airs, fugues, themes and variations and a variety of dances. The concertos were largely composed of new material.
=561  \\$aOne copy is bought at ... November 1856 (manuscript in pencil); ex Libris H. Sauntlon [?] (manuscript in ink)

=100  \\$aFenaroli, Fedele$d1730-1818$4aut$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q3740837$96147
=245  \\$aMetodo nuovamente riformato de' partimenti
=260  \\$aMilan$bRicordi$c1850
=300  \\$aiv-411 pages
=500  \\$aFedele Fenaroli (1730-1818) was an Italian composer, music educator and a representative of the so-called Neapolitan School.
=500  \\$aFenaroli composed mainly church music and works for organ or harpsichord. His fame, however, is based more on his didactic work and the success of some of his teaching materials, especially his Partimenti, which were widely circulated beyond Italy.
=500  \\$aWith the help of a partimento, typical models of voice leading, harmony, form and motives are practised. Compositional models in partimenti are above all the octave rule (scala), cadences (cadenze) and sequences (movimenti). There is no strict separation of counterpoint and harmony in the partimento tradition.
=561  \\$aEx libris Giovanni Marganti (plate), Gregorio Berruli & others (manuscript notations + stamps)
=590  \\$aarricchito di schiarimenti e di una completa Imitazione dal Maestro Emmanuele Guarnaccia, a più facile intelligenza de' Partimenti medesimi, e reso atto a procurare nozioni esatte intorno al contrappunto

=500  \\$aMs. note: "A Birth-Day Present from my good and beloved Daughter Miriam (Mrs. Henry Crane) received with the affection and additional prayers, by W. H. Havergal. Shareshill Jan 18. 1865"

Jane Miriam Crane (formerly Havergal) published records of her father's life. William Henry Havergal (1793 – 1870) was an Anglican clergyman, writer, composer and hymnwriter, and a publisher of sermons and pamphlets. He was also the father of the hymn-writer and poet Frances Ridley Havergal and the clergyman and organist Henry East Havergal (1820–1875).

=260  \\$aNetherlands$bs.n.$c19xx
=300  \\$a121-7 pages 
=500  \\$aAutograph containing performance marks (letters in blue pencil) and overpasted fragments
=500  \\$aErnest Willem Mulder was a Dutch composer, conductor, and teacher. He was a pupil of Schulz (piano) and Zweers (composition) at the Amsterdam Toonkunst Conservatorium, where he became a professor of theory and composition. From 1938 to 1947 he lectured at the University of Utrecht. He also was director of the Bussum Music School and conductor of the Toonkunst society. He is remembered by Ton Koopman for having written a book on countyerpoint.

=041  \\$aeng
=059  \\$avv orch ch
=100  \\$aHandel, George Frideric$d1685-1759$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q7302$94765
=240  \\$aHWV 74
=245  \\$aAn Ode or Serenata for the Birth Day of Queen Ann Composed in the Year 1713
=260  \\$aLondon$bArnold$c1787-97
=300  \\$a44 pages
=490  \\$aThe Works of G. F. Händel, Ed. in Score$vplate nos. 54
=500  \\$aThe ode, which lasts about half an hour, was composed as homage music for the birthday of the British Queen Anne on 6 February 1713, i.e. a few months after Handel had come to London for the second time (and now for good).  At the time of its conception, the conclusion of the Peace of Utrecht, in whose negotiations Queen Anne had participated, was imminent. The poem is therefore at the same time a thanksgiving for the conclusion of the peace; and refers mainly to the Treaty of Utrecht, which brought an end to the War of the Spanish Succession. Since Queen Anne was ill on her birthday in 1713, it is assumed that the performance did not take place until a year later.

=500  \\$aHandel wrote the Oratorio in 1708 in Rome, where it was first performed in the Palazzo Bonelli of the Marchese Ruspoli on Easter Sunday, 8 April, conducted by Arcangelo Corelli, and repeated the following day. Numerous invoices in the Archivio Segreto Vaticano show that several craftsmen were commissioned to build a stage and decorations, i.e. the work was staged. Approximately 1500 printed libretti prove that, including three public rehearsals, about 300 listeners were present at each performance.

=100  \\$aHandel, George Frideric$d1685-1759$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q7302$94765
=240  \\$aMusic for the Royal Fireworks HWV 351
=245  \\$aThe Musick for the Royal Fireworks, Performed in the Year 1749 
=260  \\$aLondon$bArnold$c[1788]
=300  \\$a20 pages
=490  \\$aThe Works of G. F. Händel, Ed. in Score$vplate nos. 24
=500  \\$aThe Music for the Royal Fireworks is a suite in D major for wind instruments composed  in 1749 under contract of George II of Great Britain for the fireworks in London's Green Park on 27 April 1749. The music celebrates the end of the War of the Austrian Succession and the signing of the Treaty of Aix-la-Chapelle (Aachen) in 1748.  The musicians performed in a specially constructed building designed by Servandoni, a theatre designer, assisted by four Italians. Andrea Casali and Andrea Soldi designed the decorations. The fireworks themselves were devised and controlled by Gaetano Ruggieri and Giuseppe Sarti, both from Bologna.  Charles Frederick was the controller, captain Thomas Desaguliers was the chief fire master. The display was not as successful as the music itself: the weather was rainy causing many misfires and in the middle of the show the right pavilion caught fire. Also, a woman's clothes were set on fire by a stray rocket and other fireworks burned two soldiers and blinded a third. Yet another soldier had his hand blown off during an earlier rehearsal for the 101 cannons which were used during the event.

=500  \\$aMuzio Filippo Vincenzo Francesco Saverio Clementi (1752-1832) was an Italian composer, virtuoso pianist, pedagogue, conductor, music publisher, editor, and piano manufacturer, who was mostly active in England.
=500  \\$aIn 1826 Clementi completed his collection of keyboard studies (100 instructional pieces), Gradus ad Parnassum, and set off for Paris with the intention of publishing the third volume of the work simultaneously in Paris, London, and Leipzig. After staying in Baden and most likely making another visit to Italy, he returned to London in the autumn of 1827.

=059  \\$a2S MS A T B 2SATB fl 2ob 2bsn 2tpt 2hrn 3vn vla bc
=100  \\$aHandel, George Frideric$d1685-1759$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q7302$94765

=245  \\$aJephtha, a Sacred Oratorio:$bIn Score, Composed in the Year 1751
=260  \\$aLondon$bArnold$c1792
=300  \\$a230-[1] pages
=490  \\$aThe Works of G. F. Händel, Ed. in Score$v116-121
=500  \\$aThe oratorio Jephtha (HWV 70) has an English language libretto by the Rev. Thomas Morell, based on the story of Jephtha in Judges (Chapter 11) and Jephthes, sive Votum (Jeptha, or the Vow) (1554) by George Buchanan. Whilst writing Jephtha, Handel was increasingly troubled by his gradual loss of sight, and this proved to be his last oratorio. In the autograph score, at the end of the chorus "How dark, O Lord, are thy decrees" he wrote "Reached here on 13 February 1751, unable to go on owing to weakening of the sight of my left eye." 
=520  \\$aThe story revolves around Jephtha's rash promise to the Almighty that if he is victorious, he will sacrifice the first creature he meets on his return. He is met by his beloved daughter Iphis. However, an angel intervenes to stop the sacrifice, and Iphis only needs to dedicate her life to the Lord. This is an unusual interpretation of the Bible story, although one which has been current since the Middle Ages. The more common interpretation is that Jephthah chooses to sacrifice his daughter, but a short reprieve is arranged, after which Iphis dutifully returns and is killed.
=561  \\$aEx libris Dr. Arnold, 1842 & William Pole, 1895 (manuscript)

=100  \\$aHavingha, Gerhardus$d1696-1753$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q55835995$96203
=245  \\$aVIII Suites $bgecomponeerd voor de Clavecijmbal off Spinet op.1
=260  \\$aAmsterdam$c18xx
=300  \\$a[viii]-113 pages
=500  \\$a19th-century ms. copy of Le Cène's ed. of 1725
=500  \\$aAround 1725 Havingha had a copy printed for his own account by le Cène in Amsterdam: VIII Suites composed for clavecymbel or spinet, dedicated to the mayors of Alkmaar, and meant as a defence of the right of the equal temperament he advocated with conviction; it is mainly as such that this opus has its historical value.
=500  \\$aHavingha was organist of the St. Laurenchurch of Alkmaar beginning in 1722. Besides playng the church organ and carillons (as did all Dutch organists), he was keeper of the city's harpsichord. During summers he kept it at home, where he probably directed the local Collegium Musicum for which he wrote music that is lost.
=504  \\$aSTIMU in Utrecht published a facsimile edition of the La Cène print with an introduction by Clemens Romijn in 1990.
=590  \\$aBestaande in Ouvertures, Preludien, Allemanden, Sarabanden, Aire, Gavotten, Entrées, Marschen, Menuetten en Gigz. Opgedragen aan de Edele Groot Achtbaare Heeren Borgemeesteren der Stad Alkmaar ... opera

=300  \\$a15 pages
=500  \\$aThe Piano Sonata in E-flat major, Hob. XVI/52, L. 62, was written in 1794 by Joseph Haydn. It is the last of Haydn's piano sonatas, and is widely considered his greatest. It has been the subject of extensive analysis by distinguished musicological personages such as Heinrich Schenker and Sir Donald Tovey, largely because of its expansive length, unusual harmonies and interesting development.

=100  \\$aHaydn, Joseph$d1732-1809$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q7349$94783
=240  \\$aSymphony no. 83 in G minor 'La poule', Hob.I:83
=245  \\$aDu Répertoire De La Loge Olympique : Sinfonie Periodique
=260  \\$aDelft$cs.d.
=300  \\$a29 pages
=500  \\$aParts used in the orchestra Muziek Collegie in Delft, Netherlands. This City Music College is part of an art society called 'Tandem fit surculus arbor' ('Eventually the twig becomes a tree'), founded in 1828 by wealthy Delfters. The parts are a mishmash from different origins: 2ob, fl solo, 2bsn, 2hn, vla and db parts are printed and all contain the Stamp of the art society 'Tandem fit surculus arbor 1828 Delft' as well as the Stamp "Muziek Collegie No 74". Only vn1 part is manuscript, and contains a title page mentioning "Sinfonie Periodique", the connection with the 'Concert de la Loge Olympique', a Parisian a concert company founded in the 1780s, as well as Muziek Collegie No 74. Only the db part has a title page, which is similar with the ms vn1 part, except mentioning "tirée de l'oeuvre 51".

=100  \\$aNasci, Michele$d1745/50-1795$4cmp$96209
=245  \\$aSonate sei di cembalo. Con accompagnamento di violino
=260  \\$aLondon$bWelcker$c1771c
=300  \\$a33 pages
=500  \\$aIn the 1780ies Nasci was a violin teacher at the  Conservatorio di Sant'Onofrio in Naples
=500  \\$aTitle page mentions "Just Published the Fairy Prince by Dr Arne ..." The work premiered at the Covent Garden Theatre, London, on 12 November 1771, which makes 1771 the probable publishing date of Nasci's Sonatas.
=500  \\$aIn 1770, he was first violin in the orchestra of the Florentine theatre, which was notoriously dedicated to comic operas. The English traveller and scholar Charles Burney, whose testimony constitutes the most important source for Nasci's biography, remembered him in this role. Burney got to know him during his stay in Naples, at the home of the English ambassador William Hamilton, where he was able to hear him perform some of his trios: he gained a very favourable impression of them, judging them 'extremely pretty' and performed 'with a very uncommon degree of grace and facility' (Burney, 1771, p. 345). The publication of a collection of Nasci's music in London in 1771, the Sonate sei di cembalo con l'accompagnamento di violino, published by Welcker and dedicated to Catherine Barlow, Lord Hamilton's first wife, remembered not only by Burney but also by Leopold Mozart as a skilled harpsichordist (Burney, 1771, pp. 321 f.; Mozart. Briefe und Aufzeichnungen, I, Kassel 1962, p. 348). It is possible that the edition was the result of the good offices of Burney himself (manuscript copies of Nasci's sonatas and trios were kept in his library); but it is more likely that it was Lord Hamilton who sponsored it: it is established that the Hamiltons were in London in that year. Burney's testimony and the dedication of the Six Harpsichord Sonatas reveal Nasci's familiarity with one of the most important artistic, musical and social salons in Naples.

=100  \\$aMendelssohn-Bartholdy, Felix$d1809-1847$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q46096$94998
=240  \\$aPsalm 42, Op. 42 (MWV A 15) "Wie der Hirsch schreit"
=245  \\$aDer XLII. Psalm op. 42
=260  \\$aLeipzig$bBreitkopf & Härtel$c1838
=300  \\$a87 pages
=500  \\$aPsalm 42, Op. 42 (MWV A 15) "Wie der Hirsch schreit" ("As pants the Hart") is a cantata for soprano, mixed choir and orchestra composed and published in 1837 (revised 1838). The text is based on the Old Testament Psalm 42 in Martin Luther's translation. 
At the work's first performance, in Leipzig on 1 January 1838, Mendelssohn conducted the Leipzig Gewandhaus Orchestra, with Clara Novello as soprano. Schumann opined in 1837 that Mendelssohn's setting of Psalm 42 was the "highest point that he [Mendelssohn] reached as a composer for the church. Indeed the highest point recent church music has reached at all." Mendelssohn himself described it as “my best sacred piece… the best thing I have composed in this manner”, a work “I hold in greater regard than most of my other compositions.” (Wikipedia) 
=500  \\$aFront matter and last page contain manuscript notes by Carl Schiedermayer about the purchase of this score from Fr. Hauser on october 4th 1850, as well as a performance on May 1st 1851.

=100  \\$aRoubo, André Jacob$d1739-1791$4aut$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q2847056$96233
=245  \\$aL'Art du menuisier
=250  \\$a1st ed.
=260  \\$aParis$bAcadémie royale des sciences$c1769-74
=300  \\$a5 vols. (1312+276+170 pages)$billustrations, folded pages
=500  \\$aAndré Jacob Roubo (1739-1791) was a master carpenter and cabinetmaker His 's comprehensive four-part treatise L’Art du Menuisier (The Art of the Carpenter) was published between 1769 and 1775 by the Académie des Sciences.The original four-volume work consisted of 1066 pages in total and many hundreds of plates which were engraved by Roubo himself. Sometimes colloquially referred to as "The Roubo", it is considered even today as the best guide for traditional joinery. It develops and discusses in detail the technical knowledge of carpentry that was under Louis XV in its heyday.  
=500  \\$aL’Art du Menuisier is divided into four volumes:
07G01_1: Première partie (building joinery part I) – this part covers basic geometry, types of wood, profiles, assemblages, tools, mobile joinery, shutters and wickets, crosses, and doors; Seconde partie (building joinery part II) – this part covers flooring (including parquetry), panelling, interior decoration, liturgical furniture, installation and assembly of joinery, arches and curves, and stairs.
07G01_2: Troisième partie (specialty woodwork) – the third part consist of three discrete sections: coach construction, furniture, and 
07G01_3: cabinetmaking. Quatrieme partie (garden woodwork) – this part covers the fundamentals of outdoor woodwork and deals with trellis, pilasters & columns, vases & flowerpots; Essaie, Conclusion, Table Alphabetique.
07G01_4: Plates
07G01_5: Plates

=500  \\$aHandel completed "Joseph" in the summer of 1743.  It is composed to an English language libretto by the Reverend James Miller, based on Apostolo Zeno's Italian language libretto for Giuseppe, an oratorio by Antonio Caldara. The libretto is based on the Biblical story of Joseph found in Genesis chapters 38–45. It received its premiere performance on 2 March 1744 at the Covent Garden Theatre.

=041  \\$afre
=100  \\$aFuretière, Antoine$d1619-1688$4aut$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q586211$96238
=245  \\$aDictionnaire universel,$bcontenant generalement tous les mots françois tant vieux que modernes, et les termes de toutes les sciences et des arts
=250  \\$a4th ed.
=260  \\$aThe Hague$bs.n.$c1727
=300  \\$a4 vols. (unpaginated)$btitle pages in red and black ink
=520  \\$aThe 'Dictionnaire universel' was a dictionary and encyclopedia compiled by Antoine Furetière and published posthumously in 1690. Unlike the rival dictionary of the Académie française, finally published in 1694, Furetière's 'Dictionnaire' included specialized technical and scientific vocabulary. It was also an encyclopedia of sorts in dealing with things as well as words. Despite having been banned in France, it was a commercial success and exerted an enormous influence on eighteenth-century French dictionaries and encyclopedias. It continued to be edited and republished through 1727 (this edition).
=534  \\$aOriginally published in 1690
=590  \\$aOu l'on explique tout ce qui concerne la philosophie; logique & physique; la medecine ou anatomie; pathologie, therapeutique, chirurgie, pharmacie, chymie, botanique; ou l’histoire naturelle des plantes, & celle des animaux, mineraux, metaux & pierreries, & celle des drogues naturelles & artificielles. La mythologie, et l'antiquité sacree et profane, la theologie naturelle, positive, scholastique, morale & mystique; la jurisprudence civile & canonique, feodale & municipale, & celle des ordonnances. Les mathematiques; la geometrie, l’arithmetique, & l’algebre; la trigonometrie, geodesie, ou l’arpentage, l'astronomie, l’astrologie, la gnomonique, la geographie; la musique, l'optique, catoptrique, dioptriquè, & perspective; l'architecture civile & militaire, la pyrotechnie, tactique, & statique. Les arts; la rhetorique, la poesie, la grammaire, la peinture, sculpture, &c. la marine, le manege, la danse, l’eserime, le blason, la venerie, la fauconnerie, la pêche, l’agriculture, l'oeconomie, & les arts mechaniques. Avec les termes de relations d’orient & d'occident, la qualité des poids, mesures & monnoyes; les etimologies des mots, l’invention des choses, & l'origine de proverbes, & leur relation à ceux des autres langues. Les remarues des auteurs qui ont traité de la langue françoise & plusieurs traits d'histoires, curiositez naturelles, & setences morales, qui sont raportées pour donner des exemples de phrases & de constructions. Le tout extrait des plus excellens auteurs anciens & modernes.

=500  \\$aJudas Maccabaeus is an oratorio in three acts composed in 1746. The oratorio was devised as a compliment to the victorious Prince William Augustus, Duke of Cumberland upon his return from the Battle of Culloden (16 April 1746). The first performance took place on 1 April 1747 at the Royal Opera House, and Judas Maccabaeus became one of Handel's most popular oratorios.

=245  \\$aAcis et Galatée
=260  \\$aParis$bBallard$c1686
=300  \\$a[iv]-li-183 pages
=500  \\$aGuillo 2021 n° 1686-K, RISM L 2932 and LL 2932.
=500  \\$aUnlike most of his operas, which are designated tragédies en musique, Lully called this work a pastorale-héroïque, because it was on a pastoral theme and had only three acts (plus a prologue), compared to the usual five. The libretto is based on the legend of Acis and Galatea from Book XIII of Ovid's Metamorphoses. It is Lully's last masterpiece and the last work for the stage that he completed.

=245  \\$aSemele, a Dramatick Performance in Score. The Words Altered from Congreve, the Musick Composed in the Year 1743 $b[HWV 58]

=300  \\$a191 pages

=500  \\$aSemele is a 'musical drama', originally presented "after the manner of an oratorio" in three parts. Based on a pre-existent opera libretto by William Congreve, written around 1705–6 and originally set to music in John Eccles's opera Semele. Handel's text was adapted by an unknown collaborator. The work is an opera in all but name but was first presented in concert form at Covent Garden theatre on 10 February 1744. The story comes from Ovid's Metamorphoses and concerns Semele, mother of Bacchus.

=500  \\$aThe Te Deum for the Victory at the Battle of Dettingen is the fifth and last setting by Handel of the 4th-century Ambrosian hymn, Te Deum, or We Praise Thee, O God. He wrote it in 1743, only a month after the battle itself, during which Britain and its allies Hannover and Austria soundly routed the French.

=100  \\$aAriosti, Attilio$d1666-1729$4att$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q758130$920829
=245  \\$a6 cantate e 6 lezioni per viola d'amore
=260  \\$aLondon$bs.n.$c1728
=300  \\$a[vi]-12-[12]-47-[5] pages
=500  \\$aAttributed to "A. A."
=500  \\$aThis collection was dedicated to King George I and members of the royal family. Its extensive subscription list (12 pages!) is populated with a glittering array of aristocratic names, (but some scepticism has been expressed by historians of the period as to whether all those named were indeed subscribers). 
=500  \\$aContains info on tuning of the viola d'amore. The lessons for viola d’amore proved popular and were republished several times.
=500  \\$aAlthough Ariosti could sing, write drama, play the violoncello and harpsichord, his favorite instrument was the viola d'amore, for which he wrote 21 solo sonatas.
=500  \\$aContains the cantatas 'La Rosa', L'Amore onesto', 'L'Olmo', 'Libertà acquistata in amore', 'Naufragio vicino' and 'La Gelosia', for which the poetry is also separately presented in this collection (Author unknown).
=534  \\$a'Sei lezioni di viola d'amore' (1724) are included, hence the attribution$ohttps://imslp.org/wiki/6_Viola_d'Amore_Lessons_(Ariosti%2C_Attilio)

=041  \\$ager
=059  \\$aS T B SATB 2fl 2bn 2ob 2vla bc
=100  \\$aGraun, Carl Heinrich$d1704-1759$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q161208$96249
=245  \\$aDer Tod Jesu. Eine Cantate
=250  \\$a1st ed.
=260  \\$aLeipzig$bBreitkopf$c1760
=300  \\$a116 pages
=336  \\$aprinted
=500  \\$aManuscript index
=500  \\$aDer Tod Jesu (The Death of Jesus) is an oratorio libretto by Karl Wilhelm Ramler. The poem is part of the Empfindsamkeit movement of the 1750s. 
=500  \\$aAfter its premiere in 1755, it was performed annually on Good Friday until about 1880, when it was replaced by Bach's Mathew-passion.
=500  \\$aUnlike Bach's Passions, Graun's setting does not imbue the tenor soloist with the role of narrator or Evangelist, nor is the bass cast as Vox Christi. The music is post-Baroque, an italianate galant style, and contains little counterpoint (notably in the duet, no. 17) or fugal movements (chorus no. 14 is a double fugue). Instead, it gives prominence to melody and voice. All arias are da capo arias with stylistic borrowings from opera arias. Grauner's recitative settings are highly expressive, culminating in the moving simplicity of the bass's recitative no. 23 on the death of Jesus, "Er ist nicht mehr!" (He is no more!). The last chorus starts quite powerfully, but then ebbs away into a mystical silence. 
=561  \\$aEx Libris Kirchenmusik Esslingen (Stamp)

=059  \\$aSS MEZ T B 4S2ATB fl  2ob 2 bn 2hn 2 trp timp 2vn vla vc bc
=100  \\$aHandel, George Frideric$d1685-1759$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q7302$94765
=245  \\$aThe Triumph of Time and Truth, $ban Oratorio in Score Composed in the Year 1751 [HWV 71]
=260  \\$aLondon$bArnold$c1795
=300  \\$a199 pages
=336  \\$aprinted
=490  \\$aThe Works of G. F. Händel, Ed. in Score$vplate nos. 165-169
=500  \\$aIn March 1757, possibly without much involvement from the blind and aging Handel, the original italian oratorio was further expanded and revised, and the libretto reworked into English. Although Jephtha (1751) is considered the composer’s true last oratorio, this third version of Il trionfo comes later. 
=500  \\$aThe compilation of the text is attributed to Thomas Morell, the librettist of most of the late Handel oratorios. In the translation, however, he was able to draw on the English translation of Il trionfo del Tempo e della Verità by George Oldmixon, published in 1737. The introduction of a new character, Deceit, can be traced back to him. 
=500  \\$aThe performance was conducted - as with other concerts of Handel's works in this phase - by his assistant John Christopher Smith. It is not known what role Smith otherwise played in this composition. He probably modified the score according to Handel's oral instructions.

=100  \\$aAlbrechtsberger, Johann Georg$d1736-1809$4aut$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q314164$95150
=245  \\$aSämmtliche Schriften über Generalbaß, Harmonie-Lehre, und Tonsetzkunst; zum Selbstunterrichte
=250  \\$a1st ed.
=260  \\$aVienna$bStrauss$c1826

=490  \\$aAlbrechtsberger's Werke
=500  \\$aEngraved frontispiece showing the portrait of Albrechtsberger
=500  \\$aDeidcated to Freyherrn Joseph von Pasqualati und Osterberg
=520  \\$aJohann Georg Albrechtsberger (1736-1809) was an Austrian composer and organist who enjoyed a wide reputation as music theorist, teaching Ludwig van Beethoven, Ignaz von Seyfried, Johann Nepomuk Hummel, Ignaz Moscheles, Anton Reicha, Franz Xaver Wolfgang Mozart, and Josef Weigl. In 1792, he became Kapellmeister of St. Stephen's Cathedral instead of Mozart, who had passed away. Possibly the most valuable service he rendered to music was in his theoretical works. In 1790 he published at Leipzig a treatise on composition, of which a third edition appeared in 1821. An English version of this was published by Novello in 1855. His compositional style derives from the counterpoint of Johann Joseph Fux, who was Kapellmeister at St Stephen's Cathedral from 1713 to 1741. Ignaz von Seyfried (1776-1841), who edited Albrechtsberger's works, was also a keyboard student of Mozart.

=041  \\$aita
=100  \\$aGervasoni, Carlo$d1762-1819$4aut$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q16268404$95553
=245  \\$aLa scuola della musica
=260  \\$aPiacenza$bOrcese$c1800
=300  \\$a2 vols. (552-[2]+100 pages)
=500  \\$aCarlo Gervasoni was an Italian organist, violinist and composer, as well as a harpsichordist, philosopher and music theorist who is best known for this treatise as well as his 'Nuova teoria di musica ricavata dall'odierna pratica' (1812; see 04G21), which helped lay the foundations for sonata form theory.
=500  \\$aThe music examples are bound in with the 'Carteggio musicale' (08B23)
=500  \\$aThe title page contains a portrait of the author
=520  \\$aGervasoni studied every form of song, instrument, and related work available, and found there was not enough written for musicians of the day. This inspired him to write about music in a way that was accessible to anyone. After ten years of hard work he published 'The school of music' in 1800, an important treatise which was, however, harshly criticized by contemporaries for the excessive simplification of some concepts and the practice of contrapuntal composition on new melodies rather than on ancient melodies of the cantus firmus. Gervasoni's treatise of 1800 is particularly important because it defines fundamental guidelines for modulations in the sonata form, defined later only from 1816 by Reicha and subsequently by Carl Czerny.

=059  \\$akbd/vn
=245  \\$a[Various keyboard pieces]
=260  \\$as.l.$cs.d.
=300  \\$a56 pages$b2 interleaves
=500  \\$aThe first part mainly contains dances (Valses, Polkas, Marches), the second mainly transcriptions of and variations on popular melodies (e.g., La Marseillaise) and operatic repertoire: piano reductions of Mozart's 'Zauberflöte' and 'Don Giovanni', 'Bellini's 'Norma' and 'Sonnambula', Rossini's 'Guillaume Tell', and Donizetti's 'Lucrezia Borgia', a piano version of 'La Folle, Romance' for voice and piano by Grisar (c. 1830)

=500  \\$aCharles Avison (1709-1770) was an English composer and church organist at St John The Baptist Church in Newcastle and at St. Nicholas's Church (later Newcastle Cathedral). He is most known for his 12 Concerti Grossi after Scarlatti and his 'Essay on Musical Expression', the first music criticism published in English. He composed in a transitional style that alternated between Baroque and Classical idioms.

=245  \\$aEight Suits of Lessons for the Harpsichord
=260  \\$aLondon$bJohnson$c1760
=300  \\$a45 pages
=500  \\$aGeorge Berg (1730-1775), was a London organist and harpsichord teacher who during a quite brief period (1755–70) was a highly productive composer, whose works ranged from operas in Italian to short glees and catches. At the heart of his oeuvre are his seven collections of keyboard music. His sonatinas for harpsichord were a by-product of his teaching, appearing in three volumes between 1759 and 1762. Technically fairly simple, these are lively, inventive and finely polished works showing an indebtedness towards Domenico Scarlatti.

Compositions by Daniel Steibelt, first 6 nrs of his 'Douze bacchanales pour le forte-piano avec accompagnement de tambourin ad libitum'. printed in Paris, 1790, chez Mlle Erard. While in London (1796-99) he got married to a young Englishwoman, who was a skilled performer on the tambourine.

=500  \\$ain album with various pf pieces, many which have interesting pedaling
=500  \\$astamp of Sieber on title-page
=500  \\$a2 verses from "Partant pour la Syrie ou Le Beau Dunois," traditionally dated 1807, a classic example of the songs or romances evoking the spirit of the Middle Ages and its troubadours, a genre that flourished during the Napoleontic First Empire. The melody was initially attributed to Hortense de Beauharnais, Napoleon's daughter-in-law. Then the musicologist Arthur Pougin named a certain Louis-François-Philippe Drouet (1792-1855), flutist at the court of Louis King of Holland, as the true author of the melody. The lyrics were written by Count Alexandre de Laborde (1774-1842), an archaeologist.

=059  \\$avv, ch, orch
=100  \\$aSacchini, Antonio$d1730-1786$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q366727$94759
=245  \\$aRenaud.$bTragédie lyrique en trois actes
=260  \\$aParis$bSacchini$c1783 or later
=300  \\$a[iv]-243 pages
=500  \\$aSacchini's Renaud was first performed on 28 February 1783 by the Académie Royale de Musique at the Théâtre de la Porte Saint-Martin in Paris. It takes the form of a tragédie lyrique in three acts. The French libretto, by Jean-Joseph Lebœuf is based on Cantos XVII and XX of Torquato Tasso's epic poem Gerusalemme liberata and, more directly, on the five-act tragedy by Simon-Joseph Pellegrin, Renaud, ou La suite d'Armide, which had been set to music by Henri Desmarets in 1722 and was intended as a sequel to Lully's famous opera Armide. According to Théodore Lajarte, Lebœuf was helped by Nicolas-Étienne Framery, the regular translator of Sacchini's libretti. (Wikipedia)
=500  \\$aPrint plates of the score are identical with Le Duc's Edition of 1783, but title page differs ("Gravée par Huguet Musicien de la Comédie Italienne. A Paris, chez l'Auteur, rue Basse du Rempart no17, et aux adresses ordinaires de Musique. A.P.D.R. Imprimée par Basset"), and a dedication page to the Queen is added.
=500  \\$aThe opera has two extensive ballets (ends of act 1 & 3).
=590  \\$aRepresentée pour la premiere fois par l'Académie Royale de Musique le Mardi 25. Fevrier 1783. Dédiée a la Reine

=500  \\$aSchmitt in Amsterdam regulary issued London sold editions with a new title page, amongst others this minuet with variations editied by Hummel and sold in London by Bland.
=500  \\$a= Arietta con variazioni, Hob.XVII:2
=500  \\$aMinuet Favorit | avec Variations; | Pour le | Clavecin. | Nro. I(I) | à Amsterdam. | chés J: Schmitt Marchand de Musique dans le Warmoesstraat. | Prix f1. Location: A Wn, D F, D LB, DK Kk, GB Lbl. Dating: 1781-1785. Remarks: E-flat major. Hob XVII:3. First edition J.J. Hummel (Berlin/Amsterdam, 1780-1781) (RISM H 3977). According to JHW Schmitt has reprinted Hummel. However, 'Menuetto | Con Variationi' on the second page makes it clear that Schmitt has used copies of Hummel's editions and only made the title page. 'Marchand de musique' used between 1779 and 1785. W1782, 99 mentions Amsterdam edition of this piece. Could be J.J. Hummel or Schmitt. Copy A Wn: handwritten: 'Imported & Sold by J Bland : N 45 Holborn : price 2/-'.

=100  \\$aBoieldieu, François-Adrien$d1775-1834$4cmp$95137
=245  \\$aLe Nouveau seigneur de village. $bOpéra comique en un acte
=260  \\$aParis$bBoieldieu Jeune$c1813 or later
=300  \\$a[iv]-162 pages$bMusic seller's stamp at foot of title page
=500  \\$aPremiered in Paris, 1813
=500  \\$aFull score containing all spoken texts as well
=500  \\$aInteresting note concerning the role assignment printed on page 1: "le role de Babet quoique joué a Paris par une premiere chanteuse, peut être joué en province par une Dugazon, si elle est a le phisique jeune et la voix nécaissaire pour bien remplir ce rôle. l'Auteur laisse aux directeurs et aux maitres de musique à décider cette question. "(The role of Babet, although played in Paris by a female lead singer, can be played in the provinces by a female Dugazon, if she has the young physique and the necessary voice to fill the role well. The Author leaves it to the directors and masters of music to decide this question.)  A Dugazon is a singer who has a voice intermediate between soprano and mezzo-soprano, a “singing actress”, or heavier voiced soubrette. It refers to Louise-Rosalie Lefebvre (1755-1821), also known as Madame Dugazon, a famous French operatic mezzo-soprano, actress and dancer.

=100  \\$aHandel, George Frideric$d1685-1759$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q7302$94765
=245  \\$aRedemption. A Sacred Oratorio in Score Selected from the Favorite Works of G. F. Händel, and Formed into a Regular Drama by Samuel Arnold
=260  \\$aLondon$bBirchall$c1797c
=300  \\$a[ii]-315 pages
=500  \\$aSamuel Arnold had his pasticcio "Redemption", composed largely of Handel works, premiered in 1786. However, it was probably not printed until years later, because Arnold used printing plates for this that he had had made for his Handel Complete Edition (1787-1797). Included are edited excerpts from the "Occasional Oratorio" (4 x), from "Samson", "Jephtha", "Semele", "Saul" (4 x), "Joseph", "Judas Maccabaeus" (2 x), "Israel in Egypt" (5 x), "Joshua" (2 x), "Esther" (2 x), "Theodora", the "Funeral Anthem", the "Utrecht Jubilate" (2 x), as well as from the operas "Ezio", "Sosarme", "Siroe", "Deidamia" and "Rodelinda".
=500  \\$afull paged colored ex libris of John King, Letcliff 1831
=500  \\$aDr. Samuel Arnold conducted oratorios at Covent Garden (with Linley, 1786-94) and the Academy of Antient Music (1789-92)

=500  \\$aXVI plates including 7 large leaflets, printed on fine strong paper. The plans are by Weiss, with fine cartridges and present Strasbourg and its construction at various times. Weiss - also spelled Weis - was a famous engraver from the city of Strasbourg, we owe him the festive book of Louis XV in Strasbourg. Here he has cured cartouches and pays homage to Gutenberg in one of these cartouches. As for Silbermann, he is surely better known as an organ builder than as a historian.

=100  \\$aHandel, George Frideric$d1685-1759$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q7302$94765
=245  \\$aThe Beauties of Händel, Newly Arranged by John Davy$b&c. &c.$nVols. 4, 5, 6
=260  \\$aLondon$bWilliams$c1820c
=300  \\$a3 vols. ([i]-112+[i]-112+[i]-112 pages) [page numbers start at 336]$bspine missing, front cover loose, worn covers but music pages are in good condition, each volume has a printed image as frontispiece, printed music
=500  \\$aThe first three volumes of the series 'The Beauties of Handel' were arranged and published by Joseph Corfe, and were printed in the more regular oblong format. These volumes IV, V, VI are in quarto format, and are arranged by John Davy.
=500  \\$aJohn Davy was an illegitimate child and grew up with an uncle, a blacksmith. "Before he was six years old, Davy selected as many horseshoes, borrowed from a neighbouring smith, as formed a complete octave, hung them from a wall, and with a small rod imitated the chimes of the neighbouring church of Crediton "with great exactness". [wikipedia]
He became a violinist in the Covent Garden Theatre orchestra and later a prolific composer for the Theatres Royal. 

=500  \\$aFrontispiece is a portrait of John Davy. Volume V starts with an image of The Virgin Mary. Volume VI with an image of the Handel monument at Westminster Abbey.

=041  \\$adut
=059  \\$aS B
=100  \\$aCamphuysen, Dirk Rafaelszoon$d1586-1627$4lyr$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q1932984$95498
=245  \\$aStichtelycke rymen om te lezen oft' zingen$nvols. 1-3
=250  \\$a1st ed.
=260  \\$aAmsterdam$bMatthysz$bI.B.W.$bWees$c1652
=300  \\$acantus: [vii]-[212]-iii pages$abassus: [vi]-[212]-iii pages$bpart books with matching vellum bindings, cantus part contains an engraved full page portrait of Camphuysen
=500  \\$aDirck Rafaelz Camphuysen (c. 1586-1627) was a poet and preacher. This collection of songs became widely used and has been printed in a variety of editions. 
=500  \\$aFirst edition with new music composed by Joseph Butler.
=500  \\$aThree volumes in both partbooks, the third volume has only text, no music. 
=500  \\$aFor each song there are two parts printed of the left page: a basic version (in smaller print) and an elaborate version with diminutions (in larger print), on the right page one finds the text with multiple verses.   
=561  \\$aEven though these two partbooks look very similar in many aspects, they have been added to the Koopman collection separately. The bassus-part had been in his collection for years already when Ton Koopman received the cantus-part as a present from his friend and former secretary Elsbeth Gransbergen. The bindings look very similar, so it seems likely that they have been bound by the same binder at about the same time.  
=561  \\$aCantus: from the collection of Elbeth Gransbergen, with a letter by antiquarian bookseller Ton Bolland (11 june 1999), with an estimate of the value.

=500  \\$aNine issues of this monthly musical magazine. The music and text are fully engraved. =100  \\$aLeigh, C.$4col$96512
=245  \\$a[Various keyboard pieces and arias]
=260  \\$as.l.$c1765
=300  \\$a36 pages
=500  \\$aIn two hands, one childish, the other adult (master and pupil?)
=500  \\$aJames Harris, an MP who lived in the Cathedral Close and who was a friend of Handel, was central to musical life in Salisbury for many years in the mid eighteenth century. He created 'The Spring' as a pasticcio, a collection of vocal music by a variety of composers, including Handel and Pergolesi, to which he set English words to make a topical story about a man who was to be conscripted to fight the French. The work was twice performed in Salisbury in 1761 and then five times in London under the auspices of David Garrick in 1762. 
=505  \\$gTubalt Lyre by Mrs Cooke Bookham (keyboard exercise) -- 4 Arias for soprano from "The Spring", stage pasticcio, Libretto by James Harris (Salibury, 1761, as 'Daphnis and Amaryllis'; London, 1762, as 'The Spring') -- The Tuneful Bird of Night -- See Peace Descent like Cherub Bright / Cocchi -- See We Trip it as We Go / Handel -- May Love your Tender Hours Employ -- A Lesson by Sig.r Jozze -- God Save the King Variations by [Johann Christian] Bach -- Air in the Water Music / Handel -- March in the Occasional Oratorio / Handel -- The Duke of York’s March

 A Second Sett of Six Solos for a German Flute or Violin, With a Thorough Bass for the Harpsichord. Mit gestoch. Schmucktitel u. gestoch. Noten. London, Oswald, (1762). 1 Bl., 21 S. Quer-Folio.

=500  \\$aElizabeth Hardin was an English composer and organist. As a teenager (c.1764) she was appointed as the organist of St Peter le Poor church on Broad Street, London; she likely worked there until her death in 1780 when she was aged 29. In 1770 she published "Six Lessons for the Harpsichord", a series of pieces primarily in binary form written in the classical style, which feature hand crossovers in the style of Scarlatti.

=100  \\$aMartín y Soler, Vicente$d1754-1806$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q377409$95850
=245  \\$aUna cosa rara ò sia Bellezza ed onestà.$bFarsetta per musica
=260  \\$as.l.$c1786 or later
=300  \\$a2 vols. (352+334 pages)
=500  \\$a'Una cosa rara' was one of the most popular operas during the reign of Joseph II. The libretto is based on Luis Vélez de Guevara's 'La Luna de la Sierra' (1614). Cited musically by Mozart in the banquet scene of 'Don Giovanni', it was performed all over Europe, gave way to at least forty manuscript copies, and catalyzed Lorenzo Da Ponte's career as librettist.
=500  \\$aThe border of the title page of each volume has a hand-colored etching

=059  \\$apf/hpd
=100  \\$aHüllmandel, Nicolas-Joseph$d1756-1823$4cmp$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q588375$97650
=245  \\$a6 divertissements ou 2e suite de petits airs pour le piano forte ou le clavecin op. 7
=260  \\$aParis$bHüllmandel$c1783
=300  \\$a25 pages$boblong
=500  \\$aDedicated to Madame La Baronne De Grant de Blairfindy
=500  \\$aLast page is signed autograph by the composer (manuscript)
=500  \\$aNicolas-Joseph Hüllmandel came from a family of musicians; his father Michel Hüllmandel was a violinist at Strasbourg Cathedral, and members of his mother's family were also professional musicians. Hüllmandel studied in Strasbourg with Joseph Garnier and Franz Xaver Richter, both of whom were Kapellmeisters at the cathedral during his childhood and youth. François-Joseph Fétis, who knew Hüllmandel personally, stated that Hüllmandel was a pupil of Carl Philipp Emanuel Bach, which is also supported by the fact that Hüllmandel counted C. P. E. Bach among the few musicians who could properly use the sound possibilities of the fortepiano. Several concerts in London are documented for the years 1771 and 1773, and in 1775 he lived in Milan. At the age of about 20, Hüllmandel settled in Paris. There he quickly gained access to aristocratic circles and the important private salons, as illustrated by the illustrious dedicatees of his first publications, Queen Marie-Antoinette, the Baroness Talleyrand or the Duc de Guines. Hüllmandel caused a particular stir in Parisian society with his playing of the glass harmonica. Among Hüllmandel's most important pupils were George Onslow and Hyacinthe Jadin. After the outbreak of the Revolution, Hüllmandel moved to London, losing a large fortune he had acquired through his marriage to Camille-Aurore Ducazan. For this loss, according to Fétis, who met him in Paris in 1806, he was later paid compensation that enabled him to live a financially carefree life in England. His son Charles Joseph Hullmandel became a well-known English lithographer. His daughter Evalina published a piano school in 1827.
=500  \\$aAll of Hüllmandel's compositions were self-published in Paris before 1790 and up to and including op. 8; only his pianomethod was published in London in 1796. His works were widely distributed in the early 19th century and were reprinted by numerous European publishers. After 1790, Hüllmandel no longer composed, but he arranged works by other composers, such as Johann Christian Bach, Grétry, Giovanni Battista Viotti or François-Joseph Gossec.
